TOEFL Reading and Listening Question Types, Distractor Traps, and Correct-Answer Hiding Methods

Question types, distractor traps, and correct-answer hiding methods for TOEFL Reading and Listening.

Amir Kooshky Collected and compiled by

Visit the Telegram channel and Instagram for more TOEFL study material.

Home

Purpose and scope

This guide is based on the two supplied corpora of 35 TOEFL Reading and Listening tests. It has three goals:

  1. explain the question types students meet;
  2. explain how wrong options are made attractive;
  3. explain how correct answers are disguised through paraphrase, abstraction, inference, and discourse logic.

The examples in this guide are entirely original. No real-test passage, transcript, question, or option is reproduced. The separate reference appendix gives only addresses such as RT_012-L-I8-Q3-O2.

Corpus coverage

  • Listening multiple-choice questions classified: 1,624
  • Reading multiple-choice questions classified: 658
  • Reading fill-in-the-blank items excluded: 1,050
  • Questions with defensible text-grounded inferred keys: 2,146
  • Indexed wrong options: 6,438
  • Reading questions excluded because the captured visual, table, notice, receipt, or other source was absent or mismatched: 133
  • Listening questions excluded because the prompt was missing or none of the supplied choices was defensible: 3

The corpora do not provide official answer keys. Answer keys and trap labels in the appendices are therefore careful analytical inferences, not official ETS labels. Every excluded item is listed separately rather than guessed.

A principle students must understand first: TOEFL asks for the best answer

Yes, this phenomenon exists:

Sometimes more than one option seems right, but one is the most correct. Not all distractors are glaringly wrong.

“Most correct” does not mean that TOEFL arbitrarily prefers one opinion. It means that one option fits the evidence and the question more exactly on all relevant dimensions:

  • it answers the exact question asked;
  • it has the correct scope;
  • it preserves the source’s degree of certainty;
  • it assigns the idea to the correct person;
  • it preserves the correct time and status;
  • it keeps the correct cause, effect, example, or purpose;
  • it covers the appropriate amount of the passage or talk.

Original example: two answers that both sound reasonable

Source:

A university tested later class times in two departments. Attendance improved, but grades did not change during the one-semester trial.

Question:

What can reasonably be inferred?

Option A:

Later classes improve students’ academic performance.

Option B:

Later classes may improve attendance without necessarily improving grades.

Option A is connected to the topic and may sound sensible. Option B is the best answer because it preserves the limited population, the cautious degree of certainty, and both parts of the result.

Original example: true but not the best main idea

A lecture discusses:

  • why urban birds sing at higher frequencies;
  • evidence that traffic noise masks lower sounds;
  • differences among species;
  • limitations of the available studies.

Option A:

Traffic noise affects birds.

Option B:

How some urban birds adjust their songs to acoustic interference, and what researchers still do not know.

Option A is broadly true. Option B is more complete and more exact.

Original example: eventually true versus immediately next

Speaker:

I will download the application now. Once I have completed it tonight, I will ask my adviser to review it tomorrow.

Question:

What will the speaker most likely do next?

  • “Ask the adviser to review it” is probably true eventually.
  • “Download the application” is the best answer to next.

This guide treats such choices as D32: the best-answer competitor. The exhaustive corpus addresses appear under D32 in the reference appendix.

Part I — TOEFL question-type taxonomy

What is a question type?

A question type is the kind of thinking the question requires. It is not the same as a distractor type.

For example, an inference question asks the student to draw a supported conclusion. Its wrong options may use several different traps: excessive certainty, an invented cause, imported real-world knowledge, or an inference that goes one step too far.

The student should therefore identify the question type before evaluating the options.

Listening question types

L1. Best Response

What the title means

A response is a reply in a conversation. The “best response” is the reply that most naturally answers the literal question or performs the social action the speaker is trying to achieve.

The task is not merely to find a sentence about the same topic. The reply must fit the conversation.

What it tests

  • question-word recognition;
  • speech acts such as requesting, inviting, offering, apologizing, and correcting;
  • indirect meanings;
  • tense, person, and reference;
  • natural conversational logic.

Original examples

Direct information question

Where can I submit the laboratory form?

Best response:

Through the department’s online portal.

A reply such as “It is due on Thursday” concerns the form but answers when, not where.

Request

Could you turn the music down?

Best response:

Of course. Sorry about that.

A reply such as “The concert was very loud” repeats the topic but does not perform the requested action.

Indirect need

I cannot find the link for tomorrow’s workshop.

Best response:

I’ll send it to you.

The correct reply responds to the implied request, even though the speaker never explicitly says “Please send it.”

Rejected presupposition

Why was the seminar cancelled?

Best response:

It was not cancelled; it was moved to a different room.

A question can contain a false assumption. The response may correct it.

Frequent traps

D20 wrong-WH drawer, D21 word echo, D22 wrong conversational job, D23 literal rather than pragmatic response, D24 presupposition mismatch, D25 time/reference mismatch, and D26 topic association.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L1.

L2. Main Idea or Main Purpose

What the title means

The main idea is the central subject and controlling point of a talk. The main purpose is what the speaker is mainly trying to accomplish: explain, compare, announce, recommend, warn, or describe.

The question asks what makes the major parts belong together—not what one memorable example was about.

What it tests

  • whole-talk comprehension;
  • separation of background from central focus;
  • appropriate level of abstraction;
  • balanced coverage;
  • recognition of whether the talk explains what, how, why, or with what consequences.

Original examples

A lecture explains:

  • how bats produce ultrasonic calls;
  • how returning echoes reveal distance;
  • why different surfaces create different echoes.

Best main idea:

How echolocation allows bats to obtain information about their surroundings.

Too narrow:

How echoes from tree bark differ from echoes from leaves.

Too broad:

Communication in animals.

Wrong analytical axis:

Why bats evolved to live at night.

Unbalanced:

The limitations of echolocation.

Frequent traps

D15 main-idea zoom traps, D14 before-the-but traps, D32 best-answer competitors, and D09 true information that is not the main answer.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L2.

L3. Detail or Content

What the title means

A detail is a specific stated fact, feature, reason, example, sequence, or result. A content question asks what the speaker explicitly says.

The answer may be paraphrased, but it should not require a major inferential leap.

What it tests

  • accurate retrieval;
  • tracking who did what;
  • distinguishing nearby details;
  • preserving time, number, and causal relationships;
  • recognizing paraphrases.

Original examples

Talk:

The seedlings were first kept under blue light. After ten days, half were moved under red light.

Question:

What happened after ten days?

Best answer:

Some of the seedlings were transferred to red-light conditions.

Wrong neighboring detail:

All seedlings were initially exposed to blue light.

Wrong scope:

All seedlings were transferred.

Wrong actor or object:

The researchers moved the lamps into another room.

Frequent traps

D01 wrong glue, D02 poison pill, D03 scope, D05 causation, D06 status drift, D07 actor swap, D08 role swap, D10 neighboring detail, and D11 unsupported plausibility.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L3.

L4. Inference or Implication

What the title means

An inference is a conclusion reached from evidence. An implication is something the speaker communicates indirectly rather than stating word for word.

The answer must be strongly supported. “Possible in real life” is not enough.

Original examples

Conversation:

The review session begins at six. My chemistry laboratory does not finish until seven.

Supported implication:

The student probably cannot attend the review session when it begins.

Unsupported leap:

The student plans to drop chemistry.

Lecture:

Plants in the treated group flowered earlier, although their total seed production was unchanged.

Supported inference:

The treatment affected flowering time but did not increase reproductive output.

Too strong:

The treatment made the plants more reproductively successful.

Frequent traps

D04 certainty inflation, D05 invented causation, D11 imported knowledge, D13 one inference too far, D14 earlier position mistaken for final stance, and D32 a partly defensible but less exact inference.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L4.

L5. Prediction or Next Action

What the title means

A prediction is a supported judgment about what will happen. A next action is the immediate next step—not merely something that will happen later.

Original examples

I’ll print the form now, fill it out after lunch, and take it to the office tomorrow.

Immediate next action:

Print the form.

Later but not next:

Take it to the office.

Plausible but unsupported:

Ask a friend to help complete it.

Frequent traps

D18 eventual rather than next action, D06 time/status drift, D07 wrong actor, and D11 plausible but unsupported prediction.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L5.

L6. Function or Rhetorical Purpose

What “function” and “rhetorical” mean

A function is the job something performs.

Rhetorical relates to how language is used to achieve a communicative purpose. It does not mean “fancy language” or “a question that requires no answer.” In these questions, rhetoric means the speaker’s strategic use of an example, comparison, question, correction, joke, quotation, or aside.

The question is not merely:

What did the speaker say?

It is:

Why did the speaker say it here?

Original examples

A lecturer explains that a new alloy bends without breaking, then mentions paper clips being bent repeatedly.

Content answer:

Paper clips can be bent.

Rhetorical-function answer:

To give a familiar illustration of repeated bending stress.

Another:

A professor asks, “Would a bridge remain stable if every cable expanded at a different rate?” and immediately explains thermal expansion.

Function:

To introduce a problem caused by unequal expansion.

Not function:

To ask students to design a bridge.

Common rhetorical functions

  • introduce the topic;
  • illustrate a principle;
  • provide evidence;
  • contrast two approaches;
  • clarify an abstract idea;
  • show a limitation;
  • correct a likely misconception;
  • explain a consequence;
  • make a transition;
  • give a balanced evaluation.

Frequent traps

D16 example content instead of function, D09 true but wrong-question information, D08 wrong role, and D32 a plausible but less exact purpose.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L6.

L7. Attitude or Opinion

What the title means

An attitude is the speaker’s emotional or evaluative stance. An opinion is the judgment the speaker holds.

The answer must capture both direction and strength.

Original examples

The proposal could be useful in small towns, although the cost makes immediate adoption unrealistic.

Best attitude:

Cautiously positive.

Too positive:

Completely enthusiastic.

Too negative:

Strongly opposed.

Wrong emotion:

Amused.

What students must track

  • praise versus approval;
  • concern versus anger;
  • surprise versus admiration;
  • cautious optimism versus certainty;
  • a mixed attitude versus a purely positive or negative one;
  • the speaker’s view versus a critic’s view.

Frequent traps

D19 attitude-temperature distortion, D12 polarity reversal, D14 before-the-but, and D32 a nearby but less exact attitude label.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L7.

L8. Organization

What the title means

Organization is the structure of the talk: the order and relationship of its parts.

Original structures

  • definition → example → application;
  • problem → causes → solutions;
  • theory → evidence → limitation;
  • chronological development;
  • two approaches compared;
  • process stages;
  • general principle → case study;
  • claim → counterargument → qualified conclusion.

Original example

A lecture first describes a traditional explanation, then presents evidence against it, and finally introduces a newer theory.

Best organization:

An earlier account is presented, challenged, and replaced with an alternative.

Wrong label:

Several unrelated theories are listed in chronological order.

Frequent traps

D17 incorrect discourse-structure labels and D01 correct details connected by the wrong relationship.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-L8.

Reading question types

R1. Vocabulary in Context or Reference

What the title means

Vocabulary in context asks what a word or phrase means in that particular sentence, not its most familiar dictionary meaning.

A reference question asks what a pronoun or referring phrase—such as they, this process, or the former method—points to.

Original examples

The evidence undermined the original theory.

Contextual meaning:

weakened its credibility.

Wrong literal association:

dug beneath it.

Reference example:

The larvae consume the leaves. This behavior reduces the plant’s growth.

“This behavior” refers to the larvae consuming the leaves.

Frequent traps

D27 familiar but wrong sense, D28 semantic neighbor, D10 nearby noun as false referent, and D21 superficial word association.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R1.

R2. Factual Detail

What the title means

A factual-detail question asks for information explicitly present in the passage. The option usually paraphrases rather than copies it.

Original example

Passage:

The coating was applied twice, with a twelve-hour drying period between applications.

Best answer:

The second layer was added after the first had dried for half a day.

Wrong:

Both layers were applied at the same time.

Frequent traps

D01 wrong glue, D02 poison pill, D03 scope, D05 causation, D06 status, D07 actor, D08 role, D10 neighboring detail, and D11 unsupported additions.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R2.

R3. Negative Factual or EXCEPT

What the title means

A negative factual question asks for the one option that is not stated, is contradicted, or does not belong to the requested list.

The word EXCEPT reverses the normal task. Three choices are supported; one is not.

Original example

The passage says a material is:

  • inexpensive;
  • flexible;
  • recyclable.

Question:

All are characteristics of the material EXCEPT:

The correct answer might be:

It tolerates extremely high temperatures.

The three wrong choices are true, which is why they are attractive.

More sophisticated EXCEPT trap

A passage says a wetland project failed because of:

  • excessive salinity;
  • invasive plants;
  • insufficient water flow.

Elsewhere, the passage says drones were used to monitor the site.

Question:

All are mentioned as reasons for the project’s failure EXCEPT:

Distractor-like familiarity can make this answer difficult:

Inadequate drone monitoring.

“Drones” appeared in the passage, so the option feels familiar. But drone monitoring was not listed as a cause of failure.

Procedure

Mark each option:

  • M — mentioned in the requested category;
  • C — contradicted;
  • N — not mentioned in that category.

Then choose C or N according to the stem.

Frequent traps

D29 NOT/EXCEPT reversal and the answer-hiding mechanism H15.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R3.

R4. Main Idea or Main Purpose

This is the Reading counterpart of L2.

The correct answer must cover the passage’s central development at the proper level of abstraction. It should not be merely a title for one paragraph, an example, the introductory background, or the concluding limitation.

Original example

A passage explains:

  • how coastal plants tolerate salt;
  • cellular mechanisms that remove or store salt;
  • differences among species;
  • possible agricultural applications.

Best answer:

Mechanisms that allow certain plants to survive saline conditions and why those mechanisms matter.

Too narrow:

A protein found in one species of coastal grass.

Too broad:

Adaptation in living organisms.

Background mistaken for main focus:

The increasing salinity of agricultural soil.

Wrong analytical axis:

The history of coastal agriculture.

Unbalanced:

The difficulties of using salt-tolerant plants in farming.

Frequent traps

D15, D14, D09, and D32.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R4.

R5. Inference

This is the Reading counterpart of L4.

A correct inference must follow from the passage’s evidence, wording, and limits. It may combine statements from different locations.

Original example

The species survives on three islands. On two of them, introduced predators are absent. On the third, the population has declined sharply since predators arrived.

Supported inference:

Introduced predators may contribute to the species’ decline.

Too strong:

Predators are the sole cause of every population decline.

Unsupported:

The predators were deliberately introduced by farmers.

Frequent traps

D04, D05, D11, D13, D14, and D32.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R5.

R6. Rhetorical Purpose

What “rhetorical” means here

The author deliberately selects and arranges material to explain, persuade, compare, qualify, or illustrate. A rhetorical-purpose question asks what a detail is doing in that argument or explanation.

Original example

The author mentions a bridge that failed during a moderate storm.

Content:

The bridge collapsed during a storm.

Purpose:

To illustrate the structural weakness of the older design.

Useful answer stems

  • to illustrate;
  • to support;
  • to contrast;
  • to clarify;
  • to introduce a limitation;
  • to exemplify;
  • to challenge;
  • to explain a consequence;
  • to provide evidence;
  • to transition to another application.

Frequent traps

D16, D09, D08, and D32.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R6.

R7. Organization or Relationship

What the title means

The question asks how parts of the passage relate logically.

Possible relationships include:

  • problem → solution;
  • claim → evidence;
  • generalization → example;
  • method → result;
  • theory → objection;
  • earlier view → revised view;
  • cause → consequence;
  • similarity → difference.

Original example

Paragraph 1 describes severe overcrowding in clinics. Paragraph 2 explains appointment triage and remote consultations.

Best answer:

Paragraph 2 presents responses to the problem introduced in paragraph 1.

Wrong answer:

Paragraph 2 gives historical evidence for the claim in paragraph 1.

Frequent traps

D17, D01, D05, and D08.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R7.

R8. Sentence Simplification

What the title means

To simplify a sentence is to express its essential meaning more directly. The correct choice may change vocabulary and grammar, but it must preserve:

  • the main entities;
  • the main relationship;
  • qualifications;
  • contrasts;
  • cause and effect;
  • important limitations.

It must not add a new claim.

Original example

Original:

Although the coating initially slowed corrosion, its effectiveness declined rapidly when exposed to saltwater.

Good simplification:

The coating temporarily reduced corrosion but soon became less effective in saltwater.

Wrong poison pill:

The coating prevented corrosion and remained effective in saltwater.

Wrong omission:

The coating was exposed to saltwater.

Wrong relation:

Saltwater became less corrosive because of the coating.

Frequent traps

D02 poison pill, D03 scope, D01 wrong relationship, and D05 causation.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R8.

R9. Sentence Insertion

What the title means

The student must place a sentence where the paragraph remains coherent locally and globally.

Coherence means that the ideas connect logically and the reader can follow references and transitions.

Four systems to check

  1. Reference fit
    This problem, these changes, they, and another method require clear antecedents.

  2. Transition fit
    However needs contrast; therefore needs a cause; for example needs a general statement.

  3. Information order
    General idea usually precedes example; problem precedes solution; a term is introduced before it is referred to.

  4. Paragraph purpose
    A sentence can sound grammatical in two places, but only one position advances the paragraph’s argument correctly.

Original example

Paragraph:

Automated translation handles routine travel questions accurately. [A] Specialized legal language remains difficult. [B] Researchers are developing systems trained on legal documents. [C] These systems are tested by professional translators. [D]

Sentence:

These limitations are especially serious when a small wording difference changes a document’s legal meaning.

Best position: after the legal-language limitation and before the research response.

Frequent traps

D31.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R9.

R10. Applied Information or Procedure

What the title means

The student must apply information from practical texts such as:

  • schedules;
  • notices;
  • advertisements;
  • directions;
  • receipts;
  • labels;
  • tables;
  • course listings;
  • instructions.

The answer often requires satisfying several constraints at once.

Original example

A student needs:

  • a beginner course;
  • Tuesday or Thursday;
  • after 5 p.m.;
  • no prerequisite.

Three choices may each satisfy one or two conditions. Only one satisfies all four.

Frequent traps

  • correct number from the wrong row;
  • date of the event instead of registration deadline;
  • event location instead of registration location;
  • included item instead of extra-cost item;
  • amount per serving instead of amount per package;
  • one condition satisfied while another is violated;
  • an intermediate step mistaken for the final destination.

Frequent traps

D30 and D06.

Exhaustive corpus references: Appendix QT-R10.

Part II — The central principle behind TOEFL options

The most sophisticated TOEFL distractors often do this:

They preserve the nouns but corrupt the relationship.

The correct answer often does the opposite:

It preserves the relationship but changes the nouns and wording.

Suppose an original source says:

After a city reduced ferry fares, attendance at an island museum rose sharply. Attendance at mainland museums did not change.

An abstraction ladder might be:

  1. Ferry fares fell, and island-museum attendance rose.
  2. Cheaper transportation increased access to that museum.
  3. Demand for a complementary service affected demand for the attraction.
  4. Economic conditions influence cultural participation.

Depending on the question, level 2 or 3 may be ideal. Level 4 may be so broad that it loses the distinctive relationship.

A correct answer may therefore look less familiar than a distractor. The student should compare logic, not merely repeated words.

Part III — Distractor taxonomy

D01. Right facts, wrong glue

The option contains facts or concepts from the source but connects them incorrectly.

The corrupted “glue” may be:

  • cause and effect;
  • example and principle;
  • problem and solution;
  • person and action;
  • method and result;
  • comparison and contrast;
  • earlier event and later event.

Original examples

Source:

A fungal infection weakens mature trees. During storms, weakened trees are more likely to fall.

Distractor:

Storms spread the infection through the trees’ roots.

The nouns are authentic. The relationship is invented.

Source:

Visitors complained about long lines, so the museum extended its opening hours.

Distractor:

Visitors complained because the museum extended its opening hours.

Source:

Researchers measured soil moisture before introducing drought-resistant plants.

Distractor:

Introducing drought-resistant plants was the method used to measure soil moisture.

Common in

Reading and Listening detail, inference, main idea, organization, and simplification.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D01.

D02. The half-right answer with a poison pill

Part of the option is supported, but another word or clause makes the whole option wrong.

Original examples

Source:

The mentoring program increased attendance, but examination scores did not change.

Distractor:

The program improved attendance and examination performance.

Source:

The device is cheaper than the older model but requires more frequent maintenance.

Distractor:

The device is cheaper and easier to maintain.

Source:

The treatment shortened recovery for younger patients; no benefit was found among older patients.

Distractor:

The treatment shortened recovery for both younger and older patients.

Student rule

Inspect especially:

  • the material after and;
  • the clause after because;
  • the second item after both;
  • adjectives such as main, only, permanent, and successful.

An answer is not “mostly correct.” Every meaningful component must be supported.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D02.

D03. The zoom error: scope made too broad or too narrow

The option reports something related at the wrong scope.

Too broad

Source:

Three of eight restored wetlands recovered within one season.

Distractor:

Restored wetlands recover rapidly.

Too narrow

Source:

The scheduling system reduced waiting times, prevented duplicate appointments, and improved record retrieval.

Distractor to a main-effect question:

It helped receptionists find records.

Other forms

  • one example becomes the entire topic;
  • one population becomes all populations;
  • one factor becomes the only factor;
  • one benefit becomes the whole purpose;
  • the whole lecture is reduced to its most vivid illustration.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D03.

D04. Certainty, frequency, or intensity inflation

A related proposition is made stronger than the evidence allows.

Common transformations

  • may → will;
  • can contribute → causes;
  • some → all;
  • often → always;
  • one factor → the main factor;
  • reduced → eliminated;
  • promising → proven;
  • not guaranteed → impossible.

Original examples

Source:

Reflective roofs may reduce indoor temperatures during especially sunny periods.

Distractor:

Reflective roofs keep buildings cool in all weather.

Source:

The technique was promising in an early trial.

Distractor:

The technique was proved effective.

Audit words

all, only, always, never, must, completely, primarily, guaranteed, proved, replaced, eliminated

These words are not automatically wrong, but the source must earn their strength.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D04.

D05. Cause-and-effect reversal or invention

The option reverses a real cause or converts association, sequence, or co-occurrence into causation.

Original examples

Source:

A shortage of technicians delayed the installation.

Distractor:

Installation delays created the shortage of technicians.

Source:

Residents near parks reported lower stress.

Distractor:

Lower stress caused residents to move near parks.

Source:

Ice loss and seal decline occurred during the same decade.

Distractor:

The seal decline caused the loss of sea ice.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D05.

D06. Timeline or factual-status drift

The option changes when something occurred or whether it was merely proposed, expected, tested, adopted, completed, or permanent.

Common substitutions

  • proposed → approved;
  • considering → decided;
  • expected → happened;
  • scheduled → completed;
  • temporary → permanent;
  • formerly used → still used;
  • future possibility → immediate plan;
  • pilot program → established policy.

Original examples

Source:

The department is considering moving the laboratory next year.

Distractor:

The department will move the laboratory next year.

Source:

The company tested a four-day week in one regional office.

Distractor:

The company has adopted a four-day week.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D06.

D07. Actor, viewpoint, or referent swap

A real idea or action is assigned to the wrong person, group, object, researcher, or theory.

Original examples

Student:

I would like to survey every first-year student.

Professor:

That population is too large. Select two departments.

Distractor:

The professor proposed surveying every first-year student.

Viewpoint:

Critics call the design too expensive, but the author considers it valuable in emergencies.

Distractor:

The author considers the design too expensive.

Referent:

Maria said the exhibition was organized at the last minute. Her brother’s visit had been planned for months.

Distractor:

Maria’s brother organized the exhibition at the last minute.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D07.

D08. Problem–solution, means–outcome, tool–function, and other role swaps

The option notices related concepts but assigns them the wrong roles.

1. Challenge presented as solution

Source:

High battery cost prevents widespread adoption. Researchers are testing cheaper materials.

Distractor:

High battery cost is the method researchers use to increase adoption.

2. Solution presented as challenge

Source:

Planting vegetation along riverbanks reduces erosion.

Distractor:

Riverbank vegetation is a major cause of erosion.

3. Symptom presented as cause

Source:

A blocked drainage system causes water to collect on the road.

Distractor:

Water on the road blocks the drainage system.

4. Tool presented as final outcome

Source:

Sensors detect cracks so repair crews can intervene before pipes burst.

Distractor:

Sensors repair cracks in pipes.

5. Outcome presented as means

Source:

The insulation lowers energy consumption by reducing heat transfer.

Distractor:

Lower energy consumption is the mechanism that prevents heat transfer.

6. Example presented as method

Source:

The author uses a desert beetle as an example of water collection from fog.

Distractor:

Studying desert beetles is the water-collection method used by the device.

7. Benefit presented as original purpose

Source:

The walkway was built to connect two buildings. It later became a popular social space.

Distractor:

The walkway was originally built to encourage social interaction.

8. Measurement presented as intervention

Source:

Researchers measured heart rate to determine whether the relaxation exercise worked.

Distractor:

Measuring heart rate was the treatment used to relax participants.

9. Evidence presented as conclusion

Source:

Lower error rates provide evidence that the new training method improved accuracy.

Distractor:

Lower error rates were the training method’s theoretical explanation.

10. Mechanism presented as purpose

Source:

The coating reflects infrared radiation, thereby reducing heat absorption.

Distractor:

The coating was designed to produce infrared radiation.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D08.

D09. Mentioned in the source, but not an answer to the question

The statement may be true somewhere, yet answer a different question.

Original example

A failed pilot project lasted six months.

Question:

Why does the author discuss the pilot project?

Distractor:

It lasted six months.

Correct direction:

To illustrate a limitation of the proposed system.

Student rule

Before deciding whether an option is true, complete:

“This option answers the question by saying that…”

A true statement that does not fit the stem is wrong.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D09.

D10. The neighboring-detail trap

The distractor comes from a sentence near the answer-bearing sentence.

Original example

The policy began in 2018. It reduced water use by 12 percent. A later campaign increased awareness of drought.

Question:

What effect did the policy have?

Distractor:

It increased awareness of drought.

That was the later campaign’s effect.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D10.

D11. Plausible real-world knowledge imported into the answer

The option is reasonable in life but unsupported by the source.

Original examples

The college introduced evening courses and weekend workshops.

Distractor:

The college did so to accommodate working parents.

The lake became clearer after an invasive fish declined.

Distractor:

Authorities removed the fish to attract tourists.

TOEFL asks what follows from the source, not what sounds sensible from general knowledge.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D11.

D12. Direct opposite or polarity reversal

The option reverses a statement, often through paraphrase.

Original examples

Source:

Shaded trees were less likely to flower.

Distractor:

Shade increased flower production.

Source:

The method did not reliably prevent corrosion.

Distractor:

The method consistently protected the metal.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D12.

D13. One inference too far

The option begins with a reasonable inference and then crosses an unsupported bridge.

Original example

Source:

Participants remembered more vocabulary after eight hours of sleep than after four.

Supported:

Adequate sleep may support short-term vocabulary retention.

Too far:

Sleeping eight hours permanently improves general language ability.

Unsupported bridges include:

  • one study → universal rule;
  • immediate effect → permanent effect;
  • one task → general ability;
  • association → cause;
  • one group → all people.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D13.

D14. The “before the but” trap

The source presents a view and then corrects, limits, replaces, or qualifies it. A distractor preserves the earlier statement.

Original example

Early researchers thought the markings represented a calendar. However, newly discovered fragments suggest that they recorded ownership.

Distractor:

The markings were used as a calendar.

Pivot words

but, however, yet, nevertheless, although, despite, still, instead, in fact, actually, rather

The earlier statement may represent an old belief, a critic’s view, an expectation, or a partial truth. Students must identify the final relevant stance.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D14.

D15. Main-idea zoom traps

Main-idea distractors are often not random falsehoods. They occupy the wrong level or angle.

A. Too narrow: one example becomes the whole talk

Talk:

  • defines chemical communication in bacteria;
  • explains population-density effects;
  • discusses possible medical applications.

Distractor:

An experiment involving one marine bacterium.

B. Too broad: the field replaces the actual focus

Distractor:

Communication among living organisms.

This could cover thousands of unrelated talks.

C. Background mistaken for the main idea

A passage opens with urban population growth, then focuses on water-recycling systems.

Distractor:

The growth of modern cities.

Background explains why the topic matters but is not the topic developed.

D. Wrong analytical axis

Source explains how birds navigate.

Distractor:

Why birds first evolved migration.

Other axis swaps:

  • mechanism instead of history;
  • history instead of current function;
  • causes instead of consequences;
  • benefits instead of process;
  • comparison instead of explanation;
  • application instead of underlying principle.

E. Unbalanced summary

Source evaluates both benefits and limitations of remote surgery.

Distractor:

The dangers of remote surgery.

A one-sided option does not represent a balanced source.

F. Conclusion-only trap

A passage explains a process in detail and ends with one possible future application.

Distractor:

A future commercial use of the process.

The conclusion is memorable but not the whole passage.

G. Generic “importance” option

Distractor:

The importance of forests.

A passage may indeed show that forests matter, but its real focus may be how fungal networks redistribute nutrients among trees.

H. Two-thirds summary

Source covers origin, mechanism, and limitations.

Distractor:

The origin and mechanism of the technique.

This is closer than a ridiculous option, but incomplete.

I. Correct topic, wrong purpose

Announcement explains changed registration procedures.

Distractor:

To persuade students that registration is valuable.

The topic is registration, but the purpose is to inform, not persuade.

Full original example

A lecture explains:

  • how bacteria communicate chemically;
  • how population density affects signaling;
  • how scientists may interrupt communication;
  • why the method may not work for all species.

Distractors:

  • “A marine-bacteria experiment.” — too narrow
  • “Communication in organisms.” — too broad
  • “The discovery of antibiotics.” — wrong axis
  • “The dangers of bacterial populations.” — unbalanced
  • “A future treatment for one disease.” — conclusion/application only
  • “Why bacteria are important.” — generic importance

Best direction:

A chemical communication process in bacteria, its possible applications, and its limitations.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D15.

D16. Example content instead of example function

The question asks why an example, name, place, study, quotation, or comparison was mentioned. The distractor reports its content.

Original example

The author mentions a bridge that collapsed in a moderate storm.

Distractor:

To describe the storm’s weather conditions.

Correct direction:

To illustrate the weakness of the older design.

Students should formulate an answer beginning with a rhetorical verb: illustrate, support, contrast, clarify, challenge, or qualify.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D16.

D17. Incorrect discourse-structure label

The option mislabels how parts relate.

Common confusions

  • claim versus evidence;
  • problem versus solution;
  • generalization versus example;
  • theory versus objection;
  • concept versus application;
  • process versus result;
  • comparison versus chronology;
  • benefits list versus advantages-and-disadvantages structure.

Original example

Paragraph 1 identifies overcrowding in clinics. Paragraph 2 describes triage and remote consultation.

Correct:

Paragraph 2 presents solutions.

Distractor:

Paragraph 2 supplies historical evidence.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D17.

D18. Eventual action instead of the next action

The option names something likely to occur, but not immediately.

Original example

I’ll email the application now. After you complete it, I’ll call admissions tomorrow.

Question:

What will happen next?

Distractor:

Call admissions.

Correct:

Send the application.

Student procedure

Locate:

  1. the final commitment;
  2. the immediate verb;
  3. sequencing words such as first, now, before that, and afterward.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D18.

D19. Attitude-temperature distortion

The option assigns the wrong emotional strength or turns a mixed attitude into a pure one.

Original example

The proposal is promising for small towns. Its cost remains a serious obstacle.

Wrong:

  • completely enthusiastic;
  • strongly opposed;
  • indifferent.

Best:

cautiously positive.

Common distortions

  • curious → excited;
  • surprised → impressed;
  • concerned → angry;
  • appreciative → uncritical;
  • cautiously optimistic → certain;
  • mixed but positive → mostly negative;
  • analytical caution → personal dislike.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D19.

D20. The wrong-WH drawer

The response answers a different information category.

Original example

Where is the statistics workshop?

Distractors:

  • “At three o’clock.” — when
  • “About two hours.” — duration
  • “Professor Khan.” — who
  • “Because registration was low.” — why

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D20.

D21. Same word, different meaning—or a sound echo

The option repeats a word or sound but uses another meaning.

Original examples

The bank is closed.

Distractor:

We sat on the riverbank.

Could you forward the document?

Distractor:

I’m looking forward to the weekend.

Is the chemistry laboratory open?

Distractor:

Those colleagues have excellent chemistry.

The ear recognizes the word and mistakes familiarity for relevance.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D21.

D22. The wrong conversational job

The response is grammatical but performs the wrong speech act.

Original example

Could you lower the blinds?

Distractor:

Yes, the blinds are new.

Other mismatches:

  • request → unrelated fact;
  • invitation → venue description;
  • offer → past opinion;
  • apology → schedule information;
  • either/or choice → yes/no reply;
  • suggestion → definition.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D22.

D23. Literal response instead of pragmatic uptake

The correct response addresses what the speaker is trying to accomplish.

Original examples

It is very dark in here.

Natural:

I’ll turn on the light.

Literal but unhelpful:

Winter evenings become dark early.

I cannot find the registration page.

Natural:

I’ll send the link.

Distractor:

Registration began Monday.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D23.

D24. Presupposition or polarity mismatch

The question contains an assumption, but the correct response may reject it.

Original example

Why did the professor cancel tomorrow’s lecture?

Correct possible reply:

She did not cancel it; the room changed.

Tag questions are also dangerous:

The meeting is Thursday, isn’t it?

A correction such as “It was moved to Friday” can be correct without starting with yes or no.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D24.

D25. Tense, time, or reference mismatch

The response concerns the right topic but the wrong time, person, or event.

Original example

Did you enjoy yesterday’s lecture?

Distractors:

  • “I’ll attend it next week.”
  • “The professor enjoys teaching.”
  • “The next lecture begins at noon.”

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D25.

D26. Topic association without conversational relevance

The response belongs to the same general topic but does not continue the exchange.

Original example

How was the renewable-energy seminar?

Distractors:

  • “It was held in the auditorium.”
  • “Renewable energy is important.”
  • “The next seminar is in April.”

Correct:

The discussion of storage technology was useful.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D26.

D27. The familiar but wrong dictionary sense

A word has several meanings. The distractor gives a common meaning that does not fit the sentence.

Original examples

The particles were charged before entering the chamber.

Wrong familiar sense:

asked to pay money.

Correct:

given an electrical charge.

The evidence undermined the theory.

Wrong literal association:

dug underneath.

Correct:

weakened.

Idioms are especially vulnerable: in store, wrapped up, took off, gave way to, bore out, and accounted for.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D27.

D28. A semantic neighbor that is not equivalent

The distractor belongs to the same conceptual neighborhood but differs in meaning, strength, implication, or grammar.

Original examples

Target:

scarce

Neighbors:

  • valuable;
  • expensive;
  • desirable.

Only limited in availability is equivalent.

Target:

mitigate

Neighbors:

  • prevent;
  • monitor;
  • explain.

To mitigate is to reduce severity, not necessarily prevent.

Common distinctions

  • cause versus encourage;
  • reduce versus prevent;
  • uncertain versus unlikely;
  • complex versus difficult;
  • influential versus popular;
  • exposed versus vulnerable;
  • practical versus profitable;
  • combine versus improve.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D28.

D29. The NOT/EXCEPT reversal

Three options are supported. Students select one of them because they answer the normal positive question rather than the reversed one.

The wrong options may be completely true. Their attraction comes from task reversal, not factual falsity.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D29.

D30. The one-condition-only trap in notices, schedules, and data

The correct answer must satisfy every condition. Distractors satisfy only some or take a value from the wrong field.

Original example

A student needs:

  • beginner level;
  • Tuesday or Thursday;
  • after 5 p.m.

Distractors:

  • beginner, Monday evening;
  • advanced, Tuesday evening;
  • beginner, Thursday afternoon.

Correct:

  • beginner, Thursday at 6 p.m.

Related data traps

  • included item versus extra charge;
  • event date versus deadline;
  • event location versus registration location;
  • office hour versus class meeting;
  • percentage versus raw number;
  • pre-tax price versus total;
  • per-serving amount versus package amount;
  • value from wrong row or column;
  • intermediate step versus final destination.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D30.

D31. Sentence-insertion coherence traps

Wrong positions often look locally plausible because they share a keyword. They fail one or more larger coherence tests:

  • unclear pronoun or demonstrative reference;
  • transition without the necessary contrast or cause;
  • example before the generalization;
  • consequence before the cause;
  • interruption of a process sequence;
  • sentence placed in the wrong paragraph function.

A position can be grammatical yet globally wrong.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D31.

D32. The best-answer competitor

This is the explicit category for an option that is not absurd and may be partly defensible, but loses to a more exact option.

How it loses

  • it is broadly true but incomplete;
  • it gives an eventual action rather than the next one;
  • it states a plausible inference too strongly;
  • it names example content instead of purpose;
  • it covers only part of a balanced source;
  • it uses the correct topic but wrong analytical axis;
  • it preserves one result but omits a limitation;
  • it is supported somewhere but not by the relevant part of the source.

Original examples

Main idea

A passage discusses a technique’s mechanism, applications, and limitations.

Competitor:

Applications of the technique.

Best:

How the technique works, where it is used, and what limits it.

Inference

Source:

The pilot study involved twelve volunteers and showed a modest short-term improvement.

Competitor:

The treatment improves the condition.

Best:

The treatment may produce a modest short-term improvement.

Rhetorical purpose

The author mentions an old failed design.

Competitor:

The design failed.

Best:

The failure illustrates why the newer safety feature was introduced.

Detail

Source:

The workshop is free, but participants must reserve a seat and provide their own materials.

Competitor:

Participants do not need to pay.

Best to a requirements question:

They must register and bring materials.

The competitor is true but does not fully answer the target.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix D32.

Part IV — How TOEFL hides the correct answer

H01. Lexical paraphrase

The correct answer replaces words with synonyms or explanatory equivalents.

Source:

The committee postponed the vote because two reports were incomplete.

Correct:

Missing documentation delayed the decision.

Mappings:

  • postponed → delayed;
  • vote → decision;
  • incomplete reports → missing documentation.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H01.

H02. Grammatical recoding

The proposition remains the same while grammar changes.

Common transformations:

  • active → passive;
  • verb → noun;
  • adjective → noun;
  • reason clause → causal noun phrase;
  • two sentences → one clause;
  • negative statement → affirmative equivalent.

Original examples

Researchers collected samples every week.

→ Weekly sampling was conducted.

Because the soil lacked nitrogen, growth slowed.

→ Nitrogen deficiency limited growth.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H02.

H03. One controlled level of abstraction upward

What abstraction means

Abstraction is the level of generality at which something is described.

Use pen as the reference point:

  • Going down the abstraction ladder means becoming more specific:
    ballpoint pen → blue retractable ballpoint pen.
  • Staying at roughly the same level means using a close category:
    pen → writing pen.
  • Going up means becoming more general:
    writing instrument → thing used to write with → object.

“Object” is much more abstract than “pen.” It includes chairs, stones, phones, and millions of irrelevant things.

A good TOEFL answer often goes one controlled level upward. It replaces concrete details with their underlying category or relationship without becoming empty.

Example 1: related products

Source:

When printer-ink prices rose, demand for refill cartridges increased.

One level up:

A price change in one product affected demand for a related product.

Too far up:

Economic forces affect behavior.

Too far down:

Black refill cartridges became more popular after one ink brand raised prices.

Example 2: access and participation

Source:

After ferry fares fell, attendance at an island museum rose.

One level up:

Lower transportation costs increased access to the attraction.

Higher:

The cost of a complementary service affected demand for another service.

Too broad:

Money affects cultural life.

Example 3: vulnerability

Source:

A root fungus weakened trees, which were then more likely to fall in storms.

One level up:

A pre-existing weakness increased vulnerability to environmental stress.

Too broad:

Environmental conditions affect organisms.

Example 4: urban heat

Source:

Streets with dense tree cover were cooler during afternoon heat.

One level up:

Green infrastructure moderated urban temperatures.

Too broad:

Nature benefits cities.

Example 5: algorithms

Source:

A hiring model trained on historically biased records reproduced the old disparities.

One level up:

The quality and bias of input data shaped the algorithm’s output.

Too broad:

Technology can cause problems.

Example 6: sleep and memory

Source:

Participants who slept longer remembered more newly learned words the next morning.

One level up:

Sleep duration influenced short-term memory performance.

Too broad:

Health affects learning.

Example 7: ecosystems

Source:

Fertilizer runoff increased algae, which reduced oxygen available to fish.

One level up:

Agricultural inputs altered the aquatic ecosystem.

Too broad:

Human activity affects nature.

Example 8: complementary goods

Source:

Coffee-machine sales rose, followed by increased purchases of compatible filters.

One level up:

Demand for one product increased demand for a complementary product.

How to judge a good abstraction

It should preserve:

  • the variables or categories;
  • the direction of the relationship;
  • the limitation or condition;
  • what makes the case distinctive.

It becomes too abstract when it loses the central mechanism.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H03.

H04. A general principle converted into a specific entailed case

What “entailed” means

A specific case is entailed when it must fit the stated principle. It is not merely imaginable or plausible.

Principle:

If every member of category X has property Y, and item A is an X, then A must have Y.

TOEFL versions are often less formal, but the logic is the same.

Example 1: seasonal mismatch

Principle:

Species that depend on stable seasonal cues may suffer when temperature and daylight patterns no longer coincide.

Entailed case:

A migratory bird could arrive after its main insect food has already peaked.

Merely plausible, not entailed:

The bird will permanently stop migrating.

Example 2: complementary products

Principle:

Demand for a complementary good can rise when use of the main product increases.

Entailed case:

More coffee-machine purchases may increase demand for compatible filters.

Not entailed:

Coffee prices must fall.

Example 3: selective attention

Principle:

Focusing intensely on one task can reduce awareness of unexpected events.

Entailed case:

A driver concentrating on a street sign may fail to notice a cyclist.

Not entailed:

The driver has poor eyesight.

Example 4: surface area

Principle:

Increasing a solid’s surface area can accelerate dissolution.

Entailed case:

A crushed tablet may dissolve faster than an intact tablet.

Not entailed:

A crushed tablet contains more medicine.

Example 5: reinforcement

Principle:

Behaviors followed consistently by rewarding consequences tend to become more frequent.

Entailed case:

A child praised whenever she puts away toys may begin doing so more often.

Not entailed:

The child will enjoy every household task.

Example 6: thermal expansion

Principle:

Materials expand when heated, and unequal expansion can create stress where they are joined.

Entailed case:

A bridge using two materials with very different expansion rates may develop cracks during temperature changes.

Not entailed:

Every bridge will collapse in summer.

Example 7: opportunity cost

Principle:

Choosing one use of limited time means giving up another possible use.

Entailed case:

Attending an evening class may require giving up a paid work shift.

Not entailed:

The student will lose the job.

Example 8: camouflage

Principle:

Camouflage is most effective when an organism resembles its background.

Entailed case:

A pale insect may be harder for predators to detect on light-colored bark than on dark bark.

Not entailed:

Pale insects cannot be eaten.

Student test

Ask:

Does the principle logically cover this case, or am I adding a new cause, intention, or certainty?

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H04.

H05. Information synthesized across several sentences or paragraphs

The answer is not found in one sentence. It combines clues.

Original example

Paragraph 1:

A city introduced shaded bus stops.

Paragraph 2:

Ridership increased most during summer.

Paragraph 3:

Older passengers had previously avoided outdoor waits because of heat.

Correct synthesis:

Shaded stops made summer transportation more accessible to heat-sensitive passengers.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H05.

H06. The relationship is named instead of the details

The source gives events; the answer names their logical relationship.

Original example

The river froze later. Insects emerged earlier. Young birds hatched after insect abundance had peaked.

Correct:

Climate-related timing changes created a mismatch between the birds and their food supply.

The answer compresses observations into:

  • category: timing changes;
  • relationship: mismatch;
  • consequence: reduced food availability.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H06.

H07. Pragmatic inference from a commitment, excuse, or indirect response

The correct answer expresses what a speaker means or will do, not just the literal wording.

Original example

Can you attend at six?

My laboratory does not finish until seven.

Correct:

The student probably cannot attend at six.

Another:

I cannot locate the form.

I’ll email it to you now.

Correct next action:

The second speaker will send the form.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H07.

H08. The final stance is hidden after qualification

The answer incorporates the full evaluative structure.

Original example

The technique is expensive and difficult to scale. Nevertheless, it remains the most promising option for isolated communities.

Correct:

The speaker supports the technique despite practical limitations.

Wrong:

  • considers it impractical;
  • thinks it inexpensive;
  • is completely enthusiastic.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H08.

H09. The correct answer preserves cautious modality

Modality expresses possibility, probability, necessity, or certainty.

Original example

Early results suggest that the coating may extend battery life.

Correct:

The coating could make batteries last longer.

Too strong:

The coating permanently solves battery degradation.

Too weak:

The coating has no demonstrated relationship with battery life.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H09.

H10. Concrete content becomes a rhetorical-function label

Original example

Source:

The author describes homes that remain cool without mechanical air conditioning.

Correct answer to “Why mention the homes?”:

To illustrate a practical application of passive cooling.

The correct option may contain none of the vivid nouns from the example because it names the example’s job.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H10.

H11. Pronouns and descriptions are resolved into explicit referents

Original example

The committee rejected the first design. This decision surprised the engineers.

Correct:

The engineers did not expect the committee to reject the design.

The answer replaces this decision with explicit content.

Wrong options often attach a pronoun to the nearest noun rather than its logical antecedent.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H11.

H12. Several constraints are compressed into one answer

Original example

Source conditions:

  • beginner course;
  • Thursday;
  • after 5 p.m.;
  • advance registration.

Correct:

The Thursday evening beginner section, provided the student registers beforehand.

The answer may not resemble one line because it combines several fields.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H12.

H13. The correct answer uses little or no obvious keyword overlap

Students often expect the correct option to “sound like” the source. Main-idea and inference answers may instead express the underlying concept.

Example 1: physical forces

Source repeatedly discusses pressure, a thin water layer, movement over ice, and friction.

Correct:

An interaction of physical forces that permits motion across a surface.

The option may omit the most memorable concrete nouns.

Example 2: medical screening

Source discusses checking several subtle symptoms before serious complications appear.

Correct:

Early detection can make timely intervention possible.

No obvious repeated keyword is necessary.

Example 3: craft and technology

Source describes traditional clay techniques combined with computer-controlled fabrication.

Correct:

An established craft adapted through modern production technology.

Example 4: organizational coordination

Source describes engineering, marketing, and customer-support teams sharing data before product decisions.

Correct:

Cross-functional coordination improves decision-making.

Example 5: ecology

Source describes insects, flowering time, bird nesting, and seasonal temperature shifts.

Correct:

Timing changes can disrupt ecological relationships.

Example 6: education

Source describes students explaining solutions aloud, correcting one another, and remembering procedures longer.

Correct:

Collaborative explanation can deepen procedural learning.

Why this happens

The correct answer may:

  • name a category;
  • name a causal or functional relationship;
  • synthesize multiple details;
  • resolve a pronoun;
  • use a nominalization;
  • express a rhetorical function.

The distractor may copy more words while changing the logic.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H13.

H14. Insertion answers are hidden through global coherence

Two positions can share vocabulary with the sentence. Only one preserves the full chain:

general idea → example → consequence → response

Students should ask not only “Does it fit here?” but:

Would the paragraph still develop logically before and after this location?

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H14.

H15. Absence is the evidence in NOT/EXCEPT questions

The correct option may be difficult precisely because it is not stated in the requested category.

It may:

  • be completely absent;
  • be contradicted;
  • use a familiar term from another paragraph;
  • describe the right topic but wrong list;
  • combine one mentioned element with an unmentioned relation.

The student must compare every option against the exact category named in the stem.

Exhaustive addresses: Appendix H15.

Part V — A practical elimination procedure

Step 1: Identify the exact target

Reduce the question to one target:

  • who;
  • what;
  • why;
  • when;
  • effect;
  • purpose;
  • attitude;
  • relationship;
  • immediate next action;
  • main idea.

Step 2: Predict in plain language

Before reading the options, complete:

The answer should say that…

For purpose:

The speaker mentions this to…

For attitude:

The speaker is cautiously/strongly…

For next action:

The final immediate commitment was…

Step 3: Check seven dimensions

For each option, ask:

  1. Actor — Who did, believed, or experienced it?
  2. Time — Past, present, planned, or hypothetical?
  3. Status — Proposed, possible, completed, or proved?
  4. Relationship — Cause, effect, example, contrast, solution, or result?
  5. Scope — Some, most, or all?
  6. Strength — May, likely, certainly, or always?
  7. Question fit — Does it answer this exact stem?

Step 4: Apply the poison-pill test

Underline every meaningful component.

The program reduced cost and eliminated delays.

Both parts must be supported.

Step 5: Prefer the weakest fully supported inference

Not the weakest answer generally—the least aggressive claim that still answers completely.

  • possibility → choose possibility;
  • tendency → choose tendency;
  • one case → do not choose a universal rule.

Step 6: Distrust effortless keyword matches

Repeated words prove only that the option writer borrowed vocabulary. They do not prove logical accuracy.

Step 7: For main idea, test whole-source coverage

Ask:

Does this explain why the opening, development, and conclusion belong together?

Step 8: For rhetorical purpose, name the rhetorical verb

Use:

illustrate, support, contrast, clarify, introduce, challenge, demonstrate, qualify

Do not merely repeat the example.

Step 9: For Best Response, identify the speech act

Is the speaker:

  • requesting;
  • inviting;
  • correcting;
  • complaining;
  • offering;
  • asking for information;
  • indirectly signaling a problem?

Choose the reply that performs the appropriate conversational action.

Step 10: Compare close competitors explicitly

When two choices seem possible, write a tiny contrast:

A is true but too broad.
B answers the exact question and preserves the limitation.

This turns “gut feeling” into evidence.

The shortest student-facing summary

Wrong options often keep the words and change the logic. Correct options often keep the logic and change the words.

Before choosing, check:

Who? When? How certain? What relationship? What exact question am I answering?

And remember:

One unsupported word can make an otherwise excellent option wrong.

Exhaustive Corpus Reference Appendix

Addresses only; no real-test wording is reproduced.

How to read an address

  • RT_012-L-I8-Q3-O2: Test 12, Listening, Item 8, Question 3, Option 2.
  • RT_012-R-T7-Q3-O4: Test 12, Reading, Text 7, Question 3, Option 4.

Question-type lists contain question addresses. Distractor lists contain the wrong-option address. Correct-answer-hiding lists contain the inferred correct-option address.

Method note: The corpora contain no official answer keys or official distractor labels. The lists below are exhaustive under the operational analytical coding documented in the guide and CSV files. A wrong option may appear under more than one distractor type when mechanisms overlap.

A. Question types

QT-L1 — Best Response (506)
